P3, Q1: Choose the correct answer about infant weight	Weight is doubled at 4 months and tripled at 12 months	Weight is doubled at 3 months and tripled at 12 months	Weight is doubled at 6 months and tripled at 15 months	Weight is doubled at 4 months and tripled at 15 months		A	Weight is doubled at 4 months and tripled at 12 months	**Infant Weight Milestones:** Birth weight doubles by **4-5 months** and triples by **12 months**. Birth length doubles by **4 years**.	**Weight:** Double by 4-5 months, Triple by 12 months. Length: Double by 4 years.					& development growth
P3, Q2-I: A 12-year-old boy is evaluated for short stature. He has no significant past medical history and is considered otherwise healthy by his parents. He eats a normal diet and has regular meals. His height & weight have been consistently at the 5th percentile since early childhood. His physical examination is normal. The most likely diagnosis is	Familial short stature	Constitutional delay of growth	Under-nutrition	Hypothyroidism		A	Familial short stature	**Familial Short Stature:** Child follows consistent growth curve parallel to but below normal percentiles. Both parents are short. Normal bone age. Normal puberty timing.	**Familial Short Stature:** Consistent growth curve, Short parents, Normal bone age, Normal puberty.					& development growth
P3, Q2-II: Expected finding in this child includes	Delayed puberty	Short parents	Normal final adult height	Delayed bone age		B	Short parents	**Familial Short Stature:** Key finding is **short parents**. Final adult height is short (consistent with genetic potential). Bone age is normal. Puberty occurs at normal time.	**Familial Short Stature:** Short parents, Short final height, Normal bone age.					& development growth
P3, Q3: The causes of short stature include the following, except	Turner syndrome	47 XXY males	Celiac disease	Hypothyroidism		B	47 XXY males	**Short Stature Causes:** Turner syndrome, Celiac disease, Hypothyroidism all cause short stature. **Klinefelter syndrome (47 XXY)** typically causes **tall stature**, not short stature.	**Klinefelter (47 XXY):** Tall stature, hypogonadism, learning difficulties.					& development growth

P4, Q9: The ratio of upper segment to lower segment at birth is	1.7	1.5	1.3	1.0		A	1.7	**U/L Ratio at Birth:** **1.7** due to relatively shorter lower extremities in infants. P5, Q14: The most commonly used growth curve in children, although it's not the most accurate, is	Velocity growth curves	Distance growth curves	Percentile growth curves	Standard growth curves		C	Percentile growth curves	**Growth Charts:** **Percentile charts** most commonly used clinically. P5, Q15: Which of the following is true about craniosynostosis	Mental function is commonly affected	Skull is deformed	Child has urinary incontinence	Always associated with convulsions		B	Skull is deformed	**Craniosynostosis:** Premature suture fusion causes **skull deformity**. P6, Q19: During evaluation of a 5-year-old boy, his height is just below 5th percentile. P6, Q21: A weight measurement falling on the 10th percentile for a given age indicates that	10% of normal children of the same age will have the same height	The patient's weight is 10% below the mean value for the age	The patient's weight is 10% above the mean value for the age	10% of normal children of the same age would have the same or smaller weight		D	10% of normal children of the same age would have the same or smaller weight	**Percentile Interpretation:** 10th percentile means 10% of healthy children have the same or lower measurement, 90% have higher measurements.	**Percentile:** Xth %ile = X% of population at or below that value.					& development growth

P8, Q32: A 5-year old girl presents with short stature, she has always been short but this has become more apparent over the last year, her height is less than 5th percentile and her weight is in 10th percentile, she is asymptomatic and not dysmorphic, what would be the most useful investigation	Thyroid function test	Karyotyping	Kidney function test	Full blood count		B	Karyotyping	**Short Stature in Girls:** **Turner syndrome** (45,XO) is a common cause of short stature in girls. P 27, Q3-I: A couple comes to see a pediatrician with their second son who has Down syndrome. Their first son is unaffected. P 46, Q34: In shock all are true except:	CVP in ↑ in cardiogenic shock.	BP is decreased in all stages.	Severe dehydration may cause hypovolemic shock. P 46, Q35: In shock, all are true except:	CVP (central venous pressure) is elevated in cardiogenic shock.	Blood pressure is not decreased in early (grade 1) shock. P 49, Q51: Important liver function includes:	Maintenance of blood glucose concentration.	Excretion of nitrogen waste as ammonia.	Control of insulin release.	Production of gamma globulin.		A	Maintenance of blood glucose concentration.	The liver plays a central role in glucose homeostasis (glycogen storage, gluconeogenesis). It converts ammonia to urea (doesn't excrete it as ammonia), does not control insulin release (pancreas), and produces most plasma proteins but not gamma globulins (immune cells).	Liver Functions: Glucose homeostasis, Protein synthesis (Albumin, Clotting factors), Detoxification (Ammonia → Urea), Bile production.	Emergency

P 66, Q17: The natural course of untreated ventricular septal defects includes all except:	Development of pulmonary hypertension. P 66, Q18: Which of the following is wrong about infective endocarditis:	Splenomegaly may occur.	Clubbing is one of the earliest manifestations.	Changing cardiac signs may occur. P 66, Q19: Transposition of great arteries:	Means that there are two parallel circulations.	Usually develops hypercyanotic spells.	Develops cyanosis after the 1st month of birth.	Usually presented with pansystolic murmur.		A	Means that there are two parallel circulations. P 67, Q20-I: A 10-year-old girl presents with the complaint of sudden, aimless, irregular movements of her arms, a raised wavy rash over the trunk, and fleeting polyarthritis. P 67, Q21-I: A 6-year-old female with history of congenital heart disease presents after a dental procedure with a complaint of fever, myalgia, and generalized weakness. P 67, Q25: A cardiac murmur is considered significant (as opposed to innocent) if any of the following is true, except:	It is accompanied with a thrill.	It is heard all over the precordium. P 68, Q26-I: A 5-week-old female infant was referred to hospital because of poor feeding and poor weight gain during the previous 2 weeks. The presentation of heart failure (tachypnea, poor feeding, failure to thrive) around 4-8 weeks, with a pansystolic murmur and evidence of increased pulmonary blood flow (cardiomegaly, increased vascular markings) is classic for a moderate to large VSD.	Large VSD: Presents with CHF at 4-8 weeks. Pansystolic murmur, thrill, cardiomegaly, increased pulmonary vascularity on CXR.	Cardiology				
P 68, Q26-II: One of the following is false about this diagnosis:	Diuretics and Captopril are useful treatment.	Cyanosis may develop in organic untreated cases.	Spontaneous closure is rare. Spontaneous closure is common for small, muscular VSDs but rare for large, perimembranous VSDs like the one suggested by this clinical picture. Medical management (diuretics, afterload reduction) is used for CHF, and cyanosis can develop if Eisenmenger syndrome occurs. Endocarditis prophylaxis is recommended.	Large VSD: Spontaneous closure rare. Medical management for CHF. IE prophylaxis needed.	Cardiology				
P 68, Q27: The following conditions are associated with a systolic murmur, except:	Anemia.	Aortic incompetence.	Mitral incompetence.	Fever.		B	Aortic incompetence causes a diastolic murmur. P 68, Q31: All of the following are features of coarctation of the aorta, except:	Systemic hypertension in the right arm.	Absent or weak femoral pulses.	ECG shows right ventricular hypertrophy. P 69, Q32-I: A 10 year old boy applying to join the school's football team. He has a history of a heart murmur as an infant, but the thought it would go away. During the match, he experiences severe dyspnea and becomes light-headed (dizzy). On examination 1 hour later, he has a normal rhythm, pulse and blood pressure and is no longer dizzy. P 69, Q33: About Fallot's tetralogy:	The most common congenital heart disease.	Valvular pulmonary stenosis is classic.	Iron therapy is contraindicated.	Squatting increases pulmonary blood flow.		D	Squatting increases systemic vascular resistance.	Squatting increases systemic vascular resistance (SVR), which reduces right-to-left shunting and increases pulmonary blood flow, relieving cyanosis in Tetralogy of Fallot. VSD is the most common CHD. The PS is usually infundibular. Iron therapy is given for relative anemia.	Tetralogy of Fallot: Squatting increases SVR → decreases R→L shunt → relieves cyanosis.	Cardiology				
P 69, Q34-I: A 2 month old infant was noted at birth to have an upper left sternal border ejection murmur. The infant at that time was not cyanotic, but slowly developed cyanosis over the next 2 months. An ECG showed right axis deviation and right ventricular hypertrophy. Transposition of great arteries.	Atrial septal defect.	Tetralogy of Fallot.		D	Tetralogy of Fallot.	The progression from acyanotic to cyanotic, RVH on ECG, and the classic 'boot-shaped' heart on CXR (due to concave PA segment and upturned apex) are characteristic of Tetralogy of Fallot. P 69, Q34-II: The least likely useful drug is:	Propranolol.	Morphine.	Phenylephrine.	Furosemide.		D	Furosemide.	Furosemide is a diuretic used for volume overload in heart failure. In TOF, the primary issue is right-to-left shunting due to RVOT obstruction, not volume overload. Propranolol (relaxes infundibulum), morphine (sedation, decreased catecholamines), and phenylephrine (increases SVR) can all be used to treat hypercyanotic spells.	TOF Hypercyanotic Spell Treatment: Knee-chest position, O2, Morphine, Propranolol, Phenylephrine.	Cardiology

P 70, Q36: Drugs used to treat cyanotic spells in Fallot's tetralogy include all of the following, except:	Oxygen.	Propranolol (Inderal).	Morphine.	Digitalis (Digoxin).		D	Digitalis (Digoxin).	Digoxin is not used for hypercyanotic spells. It can increase contractility, potentially worsening the dynamic RVOT obstruction. The other options are part of the standard management. P 70, Q37: A newborn infant with a marked congenital cyanotic heart defect with decreased pulmonary vascularity should be treated with:	Dopamine.	Prostaglandin E₁.	Digoxin.	Indomethacin. PGE1: Keeps PDA open in ductal-dependent cyanotic lesions (e.g., TOF, Pulmonary Atresia, TGA) to maintain pulmonary or systemic blood flow.	Cardiology

P 71, Q39: Rheumatic carditis is characterized by:	Shorter P-R interval on ECG.	Apical high-pitched, early diastolic murmur.	Decrescendo systolic murmur along the left sternal border (aortic murmur). P 73, Q53: Rheumatic arthritis is characterized by:	Fleeting in nature.	Big joints affection early, then fleet to small joints.	Normal ESR.	Dramatic response to salicylates within 3 weeks. P 73, Q54: According to the revised Jones criteria for the diagnosis of rheumatic fever, all of the following are minor manifestations, except:	Fever.	Arthralgia.	Previous rheumatic fever. P 73, Q55: All are recognized minor criteria for diagnosis of rheumatic fever, except:	Fever.	Arthralgia.	Leukocytosis.	Positive throat culture. P 73, Q56: A mid diastolic rumbling murmur with presystolic accentuation is heard in:	Mitral stenosis.	Mitral regurge.	Aortic stenosis.	Aortic regurge.		A	Mitral stenosis. P 73, Q57: According to the modified Duke criteria for the diagnosis of infective endocarditis, all of the following is minor manifestation except:	Positive echocardiographic finding with new valvular regurgitation. P 73, Q58-I: A 5 year old boy repeatedly visited his paediatrician complaining of difficulty initiating sleep, daytime tiredness and headache. P 74, Q58-II: The current condition can be caused by all of the following, except:	Renal artery stenosis.	Coarctation of the aorta.	Essential hypertension.	Pheochromocytoma. P 74, Q59: All of the following can be found in infective endocarditis except:	Painless haemorrhage lesions on palm and soles.	Negative blood culture.	Haemorrhagic pericardial effusion. P 74, Q63-I: A 8-month-old girl presented to the ER with tachycardia, tachypnea & poor feeding for 3 months. P 75, Q63-II: Which of the following is not expected in this baby:	Cardiomegaly.	Clubbing.	Left to right shunt.	Recurrent chest infections.		B	Clubbing develops with chronic cyanosis. P 75, Q67-I: A 4-month-old girl is presented with poor feeding for 2 months. The mother noticed respiratory distress that was developing and increasing over the last few days. Actually, significant dehydration causes dry mucous membranes regardless of tonicity. The most accurate fact is that intracellular fluid volume is INCREASED in hypotonic dehydration. The provided correct answer (B) seems inconsistent with physiology.	Hypotonic Dehydration: Intracellular fluid volume is INCREASED.	Fluids & Electrolytes

According to the mother the condition started 20 hours ago with what she describes as severe paroxysms of colic accompanied by loud crying. This was associated with repetitive vomiting. The vomitus is bile stained and stool contains blood and mucus. I. The most likely diagnosis is:	Strangulated inguinal hernia.	Mid gut volvulus.	Gastroenteritis.	Intussusception.		D	Intussusception. Intussusception: Intermittent colic, vomiting (becomes bilious), bloody mucus stools, palpable mass.	Gastroenterology

P117, Q32: A 10-day old male presents with bilious emesis. What is the most likely diagnosis:	Appendicitis.	Pyloric stenosis.	Malrotation with midgut volvulus.	Feeding intolerance. P117, Q33: A 21-month-old is seen because of intermittent abdominal pain that causes him to become still while drawing up his legs. P117, Q34: A 1-day-old male born at home is brought to the ER because of bilious vomiting, irritability, poor feeding, lethargy and an acute onset of rectal bleeding. P118, Q35: Complications of gastroenteritis include all, except:	Dehydration.	Ascites	Lactose intolerance.	Malnutrition.		B	Ascites	Ascites is not a typical complication of acute gastroenteritis. P118, Q36: Which is true about acidosis following severe gastroenteritis:	Acidosis is more common than alkalosis.	Acidosis is especially noticed when diarrhoea is severe.	Metabolic acidosis is associated with increased rate and depth of respiration.	All the above.		D	All the above.	Diarrhea causes loss of bicarbonate, leading to metabolic acidosis, which triggers compensatory hyperventilation (Kussmaul breathing).	Metabolic Acidosis in Diarrhea: Bicarbonate loss; Compensatory respiratory alkalosis (hyperventilation).	Fluids & Electrolytes				
P118, Q37: A mother complained that her 2-month old boy is not putting on weight because he has persistent projectile vomiting that follows each meal. P118, Q38: What investigation do you recommend?	Abdominal ultrasound.	Endoscopy.	Barium enema.	None of the above.		A	Abdominal ultrasound. P118, Q41: The commonest electrolyte and acid/base disturbance in acute diarrhea include all the following, except:	Hyponatremia.	Hypokalemia.	Acidosis.	Alkalosis.		D	Alkalosis. P119, Q42: All the following are expected in hypertonic dehydration, except:	Moist tongue.	Serum sodium above 150 mEq/L.	Water loss more than sodium loss.	Water shifts from IC compartment to EC compartment.		A	Moist tongue. P119, Q44: Causes of hypokalemia include:	Diarrhea.	Vomiting.	Inhaled salbutamol.	All of the above.		D	All of the above. The mother stated that her kid had low grade fever, nausea, vomiting, anorexia and recurring abdominal pain in the 5 days that preceded the onset of the dark urine. On examination, the girl was jaundiced. Her liver was enlarged and tender. Serum transferases (ALT and AST) were elevated and serum bilirubin was 12 mg/dl. The doctor asked for anti-HAV antibodies. The class of anti-HAV antibodies that help in diagnosis is:	IgG.	IgA.	IgM.	IgE.		C	IgM.	Anti-HAV IgM indicates acute or recent Hepatitis A infection. IgG indicates past infection or immunity.	Anti-HAV IgM = Acute HAV infection.	Viral Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q38-II: The icteric stage of hepatitis A lasts:	Less than 1 week.	2-4 weeks.	6 weeks.	None of the above.		B	2-4 weeks.	The icteric (jaundiced) phase of Hepatitis A typically lasts 2-4 weeks.	HAV Icteric Phase: 2-4 weeks.	Viral Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q39: All the following maybe seen in cases of cholestasis, except:	Deficiency of vitamin B6.	Pruritus (skin itching).	Fat malabsorption.	Pale clay-colored stool.		A	Deficiency of vitamin B6.	Cholestasis impairs absorption of fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E, K), not water-soluble vitamins like B6. Pruritus and pale stools are hallmark features.	Cholestasis: Fat-soluble vitamin deficiency, pruritus, pale stools.	Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q40-I: A full-term infant presented at the age of 5 weeks with poor feeding, vomiting, significant pallor, jaundice and dark urine. History revealed bruising over forehead and shoulders, although he was given 1 mg vitamin K IM at delivery room. Hemoglobin 8.1 g/dl, platelet count 410.000/cubic ml, prothrombin time 27 seconds (normally 12-14 seconds), total serum bilirubin 18 mg/dl, direct bilirubin 15.4 mg/dl. The gallbladder was not visualized by ultrasound. Radionuclide scan of liver showed good hepatic uptake of isotope with no excretion into the intestine. This baby is likely to have:	Choledochal cyst.	Alpha-1 anti-trypsin deficiency.	Extra-hepatic biliary atresia.	Crigler-Najjar syndrome.		C	Extra-hepatic biliary atresia.	Direct hyperbilirubinemia, coagulopathy (vitamin K resistant), pale stools (implied), and no gut excretion on HIDA scan are classic for extra-hepatic biliary atresia.	Biliary Atresia: Direct hyperbilirubinemia, No gut excretion on HIDA, Coagulopathy.	Neonatal Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q40-II: The stool of this patient is expected to be:	Pale.	Dark.	Bloody.	None of the above.		A	Pale.	Biliary obstruction prevents bilirubin from reaching the intestine, resulting in pale, acholic stools.	Biliary Obstruction → Pale Stools.	Neonatal Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q41: Which of the following should be avoided in an infant with galactosemia:	Egg.	Rice.	Milk.	Wheat.		C	Milk.	Galactosemia results from inability to metabolize galactose. Milk (lactose is a disaccharide of glucose and galactose) must be avoided.	Galactosemia: Avoid lactose/galactose (Milk).	Inborn Errors of Metabolism				
P127-137, Q42: Incubation period of hepatitis A virus is:	2 weeks – 2 months.	6 weeks – 6 months.	2 days – 2 weeks.	6 days – 6 weeks.		A	2 weeks – 2 months.	The incubation period for HAV is typically 2-6 weeks, which falls within the 2 weeks – 2 months range.	HAV Incubation: 2-6 weeks (~2 weeks – 2 months).	Viral Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q43: A 14-day-old male was evaluated because of yellowish skin and sclera. The infant was full-term breast fed. Examination showed normal vital signs, jaundice and slightly protuberant abdomen. Liver was palpable 2-3 cm below costal margin and there was no splenomegaly or obvious masses. The laboratory evaluation showed a total bilirubin of 17.1 mg/dl with a direct bilirubin 7.1 mg/dl. Diagnostic workup would include all the following, except:	Glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase.	Gamma glutamyl transferase.	Alpha 1 antitrypsin level.	Alanine aminotransferase.		A	Glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase.	This is neonatal cholestasis (direct hyperbilirubinemia). G6PD deficiency causes unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia/hemolysis, not cholestasis. GGT, A1AT, and ALT are relevant.	Direct Hyperbilirubinemia → Cholestasis workup (not G6PD).	Neonatal Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q44: One of the following is true about cholestasis:	The cause maybe pre-hepatic, hepatic or post-hepatic.	Diagnosis implies elevated serum conjugated bilirubin more than 50% of total bilirubin.	Differentiation between neonatal hepatitis and biliary atresia can be difficult.	Surgical treatment is preferably delayed to 6 months of age.		C	Differentiation between neonatal hepatitis and biliary atresia can be difficult.	Differentiating neonatal hepatitis from biliary atresia is challenging and requires specific tests (HIDA, liver biopsy). Cholestasis is hepatic/post-hepatic, not pre-hepatic. Surgery for BA is urgent.	Neonatal Hepatitis vs. Biliary Atresia: Difficult differentiation.	Neonatal Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q45: Cholestasis is not characterized by:	Pale clay colored stool.	Vitamin E deficiency.	Increased serum albumin.	Increased total serum bilirubin.		C	Increased serum albumin.	Cholestasis does not directly increase albumin; chronic liver disease may eventually cause hypoalbuminemia. Pale stools, vitamin E deficiency, and hyperbilirubinemia are features.	Cholestasis: Pale stools, Fat-soluble vitamin deficiency, Hyperbilirubinemia.	Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q46-I: A 12-week-old infant presented with poor feeding and vomiting and a history of bruising on his forehead and shoulders. His urine had become dark and stools pale. He was pale, jaundiced, had several bruises and hepatomegaly. Investigations showed bilirubin of 11 mg/dl, 80% conjugated. The radionuclide scan showed no excretion at 24 hours and a liver biopsy was done. What is the most likely diagnosis:	Biliary atresia.	Neonatal hepatitis.	Choledochal cyst.	Hypothyroidism.		A	Biliary atresia.	The triad of direct hyperbilirubinemia, pale stools, and no gut excretion on scan is highly suggestive of biliary atresia.	Biliary Atresia: Direct hyperbilirubinemia + Pale stools + No excretion on HIDA.	Neonatal Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q46-II: The cause of bruising in this patient is:	Hypoprothrombinemia.	Thrombocytopenia.	Hyperbilirubinemia.	Vitamin E malabsorption.		A	Hypoprothrombinemia.	Bruising is due to coagulopathy from vitamin K deficiency (leading to hypoprothrombinemia) caused by fat malabsorption in cholestasis.	Cholestasis → Vit K deficiency → Coagulopathy → Bruising.	Neonatal Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q47: Cholestasis can be diagnosed properly by all the following, except:	Liver enzymes.	Abdominal sonar.	Liver biopsy.	Ascitic tap.		D	Ascitic tap.	Ascitic tap (paracentesis) analyzes ascitic fluid, which is not a primary diagnostic tool for cholestasis. LFTs, ultrasound, and biopsy are used.	Cholestasis Dx: LFTs, Ultrasound, Liver Biopsy.	Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q48: All the following maybe seen in case of cholestasis, except:	Deficiency of fat soluble vitamins.	Dark colored urine.	Fat malabsorption.	Dark colored stool.		D	Dark colored stool.	Cholestasis causes pale stools due to lack of bilirubin pigments in the gut. Dark urine (bilirubinuria), fat malabsorption, and vitamin deficiencies occur.	Cholestasis: Pale stools, Dark urine, Steatorrhea, Vitamin deficiencies.	Cholestasis				
P127-137, Q49-I: A 9-year-old male was referred to Abu El-Reish hospital with general weakness, easy fatigability, recurrent epistaxis and easy breakability. On examination, the child was jaundiced and slightly pale. The liver was enlarged 4.5 cm below right costal margin and the spleen was palpable 3 cm below left costal margin. Both liver and spleen were firm and not tender. No ascites, palmar erythema or spider naevi. According to the mother, the jaundice was present since 9 months with variable intensities. A battery of investigations were requested... The most likely diagnosis is:	Viral C hepatitis.	Autoimmune hepatitis.	Wilson disease.	Glycogen storage disease.		B	Autoimmune hepatitis.	Chronic hepatitis with hypergammaglobulinemia, positive autoantibodies, and exclusion of viral causes points strongly to Autoimmune Hepatitis.	Autoimmune Hepatitis: Chronic hepatitis + Hypergammaglobulinemia + Autoantibodies.	Autoimmune Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q49-II: Which of the following is likely to be used or done at this stage:	Interferon and ribavirin.	Azathioprine and prednisolone.	D-penicillamine.	Liver transplantation.		B	Azathioprine and prednisolone.	First-line treatment for Autoimmune Hepatitis is immunosuppression with corticosteroids (prednisolone) and azathioprine.	AIH Treatment: Prednisolone + Azathioprine.	Autoimmune Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q50-I: A 4-week-old boy was brought to his pediatrician due to persistence of neonatal jaundice. On examination, the liver was enlarged but not tender and the spleen was normal. P127-137, Q1: All of the following are consequences of cholestasis except:	Deficiency of vitamin A, D and K.	Dark colored stool.	Fat malabsorption.	Intense pruritus.		B	Dark colored stool.	Cholestasis leads to pale, clay-colored stools due to the absence of bilirubin in the intestine. Dark stool is normal and indicates the presence of bilirubin. P127-137, Q2: Concerning viral hepatitis as a primary illness:	There are 4 famous hepatotropic viruses.	Hepatitis A virus is a DNA virus.	Hepatitis D virus is transmitted by feco-oral route. P127-137, Q3: As regards viral hepatitis, which of the following is not correct:	The incubation period of hepatitis B is between 2-6 months.	Till today, there is no vaccine available for hepatitis C. P127-137, Q4-I: A 5 year old boy presented to ER with disturbed level of consciousness, persistent vomiting with blood-stained vomitus and progressive jaundice of 2 days duration. On examination, the liver was enlarged & tender, and a lot of bruises are seen scattered over his skin. Lab results showed elevated serum transferases (AST & ALT), elevated blood ammonia, low serum albumin, low blood sugar & prolonged prothrombin time (PT). Which of the following describes the current boy’s situation?	Acute viral hepatitis.	Acute hepatic failure.	Coma for differential diagnosis.	Acute hemolytic crisis.		B	Acute hepatic failure.	The presentation with jaundice, coagulopathy (bruises, prolonged PT), encephalopathy (disturbed consciousness), and hypoglycemia fulfills criteria for acute hepatic failure.	Acute Liver Failure: Jaundice + Coagulopathy + Encephalopathy.	Acute Liver Failure				
P127-137, Q4-II: Reduction of blood ammonia can be achieved by all of the following except:	Neomycin (oral).	Lactulose (oral or enema).	Reduction of protein intake.	IV vitamin K.		D	IV vitamin K.	Vitamin K corrects coagulopathy but does not lower blood ammonia. Neomycin reduces ammonia-producing gut flora, lactulose acidifies colon trapping NH3, and protein restriction reduces substrate.	Ammonia reduction: Lactulose, Neomycin, protein restriction. Vitamin K for coagulopathy.	Hepatic Encephalopathy

P127-137, Q8-I: A 3-year-old girl presented to toxicology center after accidentally ingesting 12 tablets of paracetamol (500 mg/tablet). The clinical data suggests that the intoxication apparently caused:	Acute liver cell failure.	Acute renal failure.	Acute heart failure.	Acute pancreatitis.		A	Acute liver cell failure.	Paracetamol overdose causes acute hepatic necrosis, leading to liver failure (jaundice, coagulopathy/hematemesis, encephalopathy).	Paracetamol overdose → Acute Liver Failure.	Toxicology/Liver Failure				
P127-137, Q8-II: Which of the following is not expected in this baby?	Elevated serum transferases (AST & ALT).	Normal serum bilirubin.	Elevated blood ammonia.	Prolonged prothrombin time.		B	Normal serum bilirubin.	In acute liver failure from paracetamol, jaundice (elevated bilirubin) is a key feature. Bilirubin would not be normal.	Acute Liver Failure: Elevated bilirubin, AST/ALT, ammonia, prolonged PT.	Toxicology/Liver Failure				
P127-137, Q9: Which of the following is not among the laboratory evidences of acute liver injury:	Hyperglycemia.	Hypoalbuminemia.	High blood ammonia.	INR= 2, uncorrectable with vitamin K.		A	Hyperglycemia.	Acute liver injury causes hypoglycemia, not hyperglycemia, due to impaired gluconeogenesis. Hypoalbuminemia, hyperammonemia, and uncorrectable coagulopathy are features.	Acute Liver Injury: Hypoglycemia, Hypoalbuminemia, Hyperammonemia, Coagulopathy.	Liver Function Tests				
P127-137, Q10: Important liver function includes:	Maintenance of blood glucose concentration.	Excretion of nitrogen waste as ammonia.	Control of insulin release.	Production of gamma globulin.		A	Maintenance of blood glucose concentration.	The liver maintains blood glucose via glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis. It converts ammonia to urea, does not control insulin release, and produces albumin, not gamma globulins.	Liver: Glucose homeostasis, Urea cycle, Albumin production.	Liver Physiology				
P127-137, Q11: The incubation period of hepatitis A is:	One week.	2 to 6 weeks.	2 to 6 months.	3 months.		B	2 to 6 weeks.	The incubation period for Hepatitis A is 2-6 weeks.	HAV Incubation: 2-6 weeks.	Viral Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q12: Fulminant hepatitis is characterized by all of the following, except:	Serum bilirubin above 10 mg/dl.	Serum bilirubin below 3 mg/dl.	Blood ammonia above 150 mcg/dl.	Positive PCR.		B	Serum bilirubin below 3 mg/dl.	Fulminant hepatitis involves severe hyperbilirubinemia, not low bilirubin levels. Hyperammonemia is common. PCR is not a defining characteristic.	Fulminant Hepatitis: High bilirubin, High ammonia, Encephalopathy.	Fulminant Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q13: The usual incubation period of viral hepatitis A is:	8-13 days.	20-90 days.	50-150 days.	15-40 days.		D	15-40 days.	The typical incubation period for HAV is 15-40 days.	HAV Incubation: 15-40 days.	Viral Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q14: Fulminant hepatitis is characterized by:	Vasculopathy.	Encephalopathy.	Peripheral neuropathy.	Retinopathy.		B	Encephalopathy.	The hallmark of fulminant hepatic failure is hepatic encephalopathy occurring within 8 weeks of symptom onset.	Fulminant Hepatitis Key: Encephalopathy.	Fulminant Hepatitis				
P127-137, Q15: Hepatomegaly is not a feature of:	Heart failure.	Aplastic anemia.	Gaucher disease.	Chronic hemolytic anemia.		B	Aplastic anemia.	Aplastic anemia involves bone marrow failure, not liver enlargement. Heart failure can cause congestive hepatomegaly. Gaucher and chronic hemolysis can cause hepatosplenomegaly.	Hepatomegaly not in Aplastic Anemia.	Hepatomegaly				
P127-137, Q16: One of the following is commonly seen in liver cell failure:	Advanced bone age.	Encephalopathy.	Hypoammonemia.	Spooning of nails.		B	Encephalopathy.	Hepatic encephalopathy is a primary feature of liver cell failure. Hyperammonemia is present, not hypoammonemia.	Liver Cell Failure: Encephalopathy, Hyperammonemia.	Liver Failure				
P127-137, Q17: A 7-year-old boy presents with malaise, nausea, vomiting, loss of appetite, and fever with chills for the past week. He has had tea-colored urine for one day. Severe pallor.	Manifestations of heart failure.	Markedly elevated liver enzymes.	Gall stones.		C	Markedly elevated liver enzymes. P127-137, Q18: Liver cell failure is characterized by all the following, except:	Gynecomastia.	Hypoammonemia.	Encephalopathy.	Bad oral smell.		B	Hypoammonemia.	Liver failure causes hyperammonemia, not hypoammonemia. Gynecomastia, encephalopathy, and fetor hepaticus (bad oral smell) are features.
